  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_919e7962-56f0-40e8-aae3-157180056a2a'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer17037864140229356657">Clássico da literatura brasileira, este texto de Machado de Assis continua sendo, cento e trinta anos depois de sua publicação original, uma das mais devastadoras observações sobre a insanidade a que pode chegar a ciência. Tão palpitante quanto de leitura prazerosa, O alienista é uma dessas joias da ficção da literatura mundial.<br/>Médico, Simão Bacamarte passa a se interessar</span>
  <span id="freeText17037864140229356657" style="display:none">Clássico da literatura brasileira, este texto de Machado de Assis continua sendo, cento e trinta anos depois de sua publicação original, uma das mais devastadoras observações sobre a insanidade a que pode chegar a ciência. Tão palpitante quanto de leitura prazerosa, O alienista é uma dessas joias da ficção da literatura mundial.<br />Médico, Simão Bacamarte passa a se interessar pela psiquiatria, iniciando um estudo sobre a loucura em Itaguaí, onde funda a Casa Verde - um típico hospício oitocentista -, arregimentando cobaias humanas para seus experimentos. O que se segue é uma história surpreendente e atual em seu debate sobre desvios e normalidade, loucura e razão.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17037864140229356657" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_446130181" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/446130181" />
    <a title="karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45618-karen"><img alt="karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596291582p2/45618.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/446130181?book_show_action=true">Nov 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="karen" href="/user/show/45618-karen">karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781612191072" href="/book/show/13155523-the-alienist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=not-a-polyglot">not-a-polyglot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=distant-lands">distant-lands</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=littry-fiction">littry-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer446130181" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2237694229963873625">ah, machado.<br/><br/>he just never fails.<br/><br/>this is just a short little work, part of melville house's wonderful "art of the novella" series, but it is machado, so you know it is going to be wholly satisfying, despite being only 86 pages.<br/><br/>this one is about... madness. and how you don't want one person in charge of determining who is crazy and who is not. especially not this man:<br/><br/><i>In his fortieth year Bacamarte married the widow of a circuit judge. Her name was Dona Evarista da Costa e Mascarenhas, and she was </i></span>
  <span id="freeText2237694229963873625" style="display:none">ah, machado.<br><br>he just never fails.<br><br>this is just a short little work, part of melville house's wonderful "art of the novella" series, but it is machado, so you know it is going to be wholly satisfying, despite being only 86 pages.<br><br>this one is about... madness. and how you don't want one person in charge of determining who is crazy and who is not. especially not this man:<br><br><i>In his fortieth year Bacamarte married the widow of a circuit judge. Her name was Dona Evarista da Costa e Mascarenhas, and she was neither beautiful nor charming. One of his uncles, an outspoken man, asked him why he had not selected a more attractive woman. The doctor replied that Dona Evarista enjoyed perfect digestion, excellent eyesight, and normal blood pressure; she had had no serious illnesses and her urinalysis was negative. It was likely she would give him healthy, robust children. If, in addition to her physiological accomplishments, Dona Evarista possessed a face composed of features neither individually pretty nor mutually compatible, he thanked God for it, for he would not be tempted to sacrifice his scientific pursuits for the contemplation of his wife's attractions.</i><br><br>because any man who is that clinical and impassive, that removed from ordinary human sentiment, is not the person i want telling me what sanity is. and by the end, you will be wishing he had just married a hotter wife. this is a man who needs distractions.<br><br>alas, instead of enjoying marital bliss with a fascinating woman, bacamarte instead sets out to single-mindedly diagnose the citizens of itaguai with their heretofore unrecognized mental disorders, and to open the first asylum in brazil. <br>he has a huge madhouse, and many rooms to fill.<br><br>and fill it he shall, using the "i don't know what it is, but i know it when i see it" argument.<br><br>machado wrote this in 1881, and the field of psychotherapy still had a ways to go, and i swear this man had some robert johnson thing going on, because his prescience, in all of his works, is so keen. he feels so damn modern, it thrills me.<br><br>in his hands, this becomes darkly absurd, and for a tiny little work, there are several reversals and more plot than you would expect, more plot than many authors could have delivered.<br><br>to be clear,this book is not about capital-e evil, it is not about taking advantage of position and influence. it is about misguidedness, pure and simple. so it is a more nuanced work than one that is just "this man is abusing his power." when you think of machado's contemporaries like dickens or wilkie collins, and how polarized their good/evil characters are, machado's psychological insights are even more impressive. thomas hardy was probably the only person writing at the same time who wrote emotional and moral ambiguity as well as machado. <br><br>and you know how i feel about him.<br><br>definitely read machado. i plan on doing some re-reading of him this year myself, and reading some of the ones i have not yet gotten to.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528725630i/25720315.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><a href="http://bloggycomelately.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">come to my blog!</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2237694229963873625" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_653041473" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/653041473" />
    <a title="s.penkevich" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6431467-s-penkevich"><img alt="s.penkevich" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587089836p2/6431467.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/653041473?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="s.penkevich" class="user" itemprop="url" name="s.penkevich" href="/user/show/6431467-s-penkevich">s.penkevich</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781612191072" href="/book/show/13155523-the-alienist">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Madmen</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to s.penkevich by: </span>
      Mike Puma
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=mike_puma_made_me_do_it">mike_puma_made_me_do_it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=brazil">brazil</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=satire">satire</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer653041473" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12807722038982740">‘<i>Till now, madness has been thought a small island in an ocean of sanity. I am beginning to suspect that it is not an island at all but a continent.</i>’<br/><br/>Each of us are wired differently and set forth out into the world with our own baggage of experiences and perspectives, our own unique defects and demons, to interact with one another. Celebrated Brazilian author Machado de Assis’ 1882 novella <i>The Alienist</i> investigates the lines where normality and quirkiness tip over into the category of madness, e</span>
  <span id="freeText12807722038982740" style="display:none">‘<i>Till now, madness has been thought a small island in an ocean of sanity. I am beginning to suspect that it is not an island at all but a continent.</i>’<br><br>Each of us are wired differently and set forth out into the world with our own baggage of experiences and perspectives, our own unique defects and demons, to interact with one another. Celebrated Brazilian author Machado de Assis’ 1882 novella <i>The Alienist</i> investigates the lines where normality and quirkiness tip over into the category of madness, exposing how each of us are mad in our own ways. Famous physician Simão Bacamarte causes quite a stir when he opens up his asylum, the Green House, in his tiny hometown of Itaguai, Brazil. ‘<i>The idea of having madmen live together in the same house seemed itself to be a symptom of madness.</i>’ However, the local government gives him full control and financially backs him, thus beginning his process of selecting any townspeople he wants to place in his own custody for psychological evaluation. Constructed as an exposé of the fictional history surrounding a town being rounded up by the head of the local asylum, Machado de Assis explores the roles of a scientific community and it’s interaction with politics as well as examines the human consciousness. Brilliantly succinct and engaging, <i>The Alienist</i> takes a comical satiric aim at psychology and revolution while managing to remain as poignant and feel as modern as it did when first published over a century ago.<br><br>Bacamarte is a clear representative of no-nonsense, indifferent science, with his entire life being in the name of scientific advancement. This is a man who marries his wife because ‘<i>Dona Evarista enjoyed perfect digestion, excellent eyesight, and normal blood pressure; she had had no serious illnesses and her urinalysis was negative,</i>’ as well as not being attractive enough to distract him from his work. The character’s of de Assis are more the embodiments of ideas than full-fledged characters in the traditional sense, however, this works in the novella’s favor. This witty satire is told from a distance, speedily recounting the events that transpired in Itaguai with respect to the Green House, allowing de Assis to keep a fast progression and paint in broad strokes without seeming neglectful of minor details. This pacing and simplicity creates a linguistic ambience akin to a parable, yet, if <i>The Alienist</i> is to be read as an allegorical novella, then it is one content to stand boldly bare of any metaphoric veils masking its naked truths. He bluntly addresses his quandaries of psychological investigation and political upheaval, furbishing them instead with comedic properties to expose their absurdities.<br><br>The selection of patients to stay at the Green House is entirely subject to Bacamarte’s will. Anyone who displays even the slightest aberration from bland normality catches his attention, and he is always able to find ‘scientific’ evidence as to how this behavior may be related to some sort of mental illness. I was reminded of an experiment we had discussed in an Abnormal Psychology course I took a few years back. In the 1970’s, psychologist David Rosenhan conducted an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rosenhan_experiment" rel="nofollow noopener">experiment</a> that involved people being admitted to a hospital claiming to hear voices. The patients stayed an average of 19 days, each being diagnosed with schizophrenia and given antipsychotics. This experiment exposed the dehumanization experienced by the patients, as well as the various hospitals inability to distinguish between actual disorders and fakers. A major point made by my professor was the dangers of premature diagnosis, or misdiagnosis, as it can often lead to looking for evidence to support the diagnosis instead of properly addressing all the aspects of a patient. For example, doctors noticed that one of the test patients was taking notes about his stay in the hospital, and assumed it was some pathological writing behavior, further compounding their belief that he was in fact suffering from schizophrenia. These ideas are reflected in Bacamarte’s selection of patients. A local rich man strikes the physician as odd because the rich man often stands outside admiring his stone mansion, and after reading an article about an illness that causes people to have a catatonic state (the early scientific journal he reads phrases it as adopting the posture of a stone), decides this behavior may be indicative of a disorder. <blockquote>
  <i>Poor Mateus noticed only that he was an object of the curiosity or admiration of the most important figure in Itaguai. He intensified the nobility of his expression, the stateliness of his pose…Alas! He was merely helping to condemn himself. The next day he was admitted to the Green House.</i>
</blockquote>One by one, then in large groups, Bacamarte finds a reason (or, perhaps, a motive, as many of the townsfolk suggest) to place them in his asylum. <br><br>An issue accrues for Bacamarte as to where to draw the lines between a normal aberration or clinical disorder.<blockquote>
  <i>First, that he had checked the statistics and had found that four-fifths of the population of Itaguai was in the Green House; seconds, that this disproportionately large number of patients had led him to reexamine his fundamental theory of mental illness, a theory that classified as sick all people who were mentaly unbalanced; third, that as a consequence of this reexamination in the light of the statistics he had concluded not only that his theory was unsound but also that the exactly contrary doctrine was true—that is, that normality lay in a lack of equilibrium and that the abnormal, the really sick, were the well balanced.</i>
</blockquote>Not only does this passage reflect the intricate sentence structure that the author is able to seemingly effortlessly construct with such excellent flow and balance, but asks the question of ‘what is really normal?’ Perhaps normality is an unreachable ideal and we must take pride in our differences, our quirks, and our unique realms of perspectives on the world. However, there are those who are such outliers that attention truly is warranted. In the early days of psychology, discovering which people were the real outliers must have been an interesting process.<br><br>What is truly exciting in <i>The Alientist</i> is de Assis accounts of revolution. The townspeople grow angry with all their fellow friends being thrown into the Green House and declare it the ‘<i>Bastille of human reason,</i>’ and organize to overthrow the local government that supports it. With each rebellion and reordering of political power, those in control eventually side with the very rhetoric they used to inspire the rebellion. After overthrowing the first local government for supporting the Green House, the new totalitarian regime approaches Bacamarte to use his selection to dispose of political opponents, and then is overthrown for this same reason by another opponent who then tries to align himself with the physician. Machado de Assis makes a mockery of these political leaders, and Bacamarte views those who struggle for political power as being excellent subjects to study for possibilities of megalomania and arrogance. Society as a whole becomes a bottomless well for de Assis’ satire— in light of modern critiques of drug companies getting rich off the disorders of the public, it is amusing that the physicians closest friend and first supporter is the local pharmacist.<br><br>Wildly comic and engaging, this quick little novella has a wealth of ideas to ponder. Despite being over a century old, and despite the leaps and bounds that have occurred in all the scientific fields, this story feels just as poignant in the modern day. If a reader was unaware of the publication date, it would be easy to assume this is an allegory of modern issues set in the past (a past that is painted in broad enough strokes to hardly betray the time it is set in). The Melville House Hybrid Book publication is also very beneficial to any literary enthusiast, offering links to read up about the author as well as read other bits of writing by him. This is a fantastic little satire from a novelist who was the precursor to many of the great Latin American authors, and one that makes a reader question just what makes anything ‘normal’.<br><b>4/5</b><br><br><i>He possessed wisdom, patience, tolerance, truthfulness, loyalty, and moral fortitude; all the qualities tjat go to make an utter madman.</i><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380427071i/853702._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="12807722038982740" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Mike Puma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4193758-mike-puma"><img alt="Mike Puma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434667514p2/4193758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/421549112?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer17360844025088353814"><p>“Sanity is a cozy lie.”—Susan Sontag.</p><p>Ever notice that even when you think you know where a story’s going, you really don’t anticipate the road it will take to get there? Just how many left turns you’ll have to make? How many times you’ll have to start over?</p><p>Not start the story over. This one isn’t that complicated. Except that it is. Not hard to read, mind you, just more complicated.</p><p>In a Brazilian community of indeterminate size toward the end of the 19th century, the progress of science confront</p></span>
  <span id="freeText17360844025088353814" style="display:none"><p>“Sanity is a cozy lie.”—Susan Sontag.</p><p>Ever notice that even when you think you know where a story’s going, you really don’t anticipate the road it will take to get there? Just how many left turns you’ll have to make? How many times you’ll have to start over?</p><p>Not start the story over. This one isn’t that complicated. Except that it is. Not hard to read, mind you, just more complicated.</p><p>In a Brazilian community of indeterminate size toward the end of the 19th century, the progress of science confronts the reality of day-to-day life with all the potential of a horror story but confined to the more practical arena of the absurd.</p><p>I know of at least one more review of this one, forthcoming, and which will be much better than this teaser. Meanwhile, 4-stars, only because, eventually, you’ll likely know where it’s going, because all roads lead to <s>Rome</s> that place they’re headed.</p><p>________________________________</p><p>I should add that the Melville House 'hybridbook' I read has one of those nifty little, pixilated cubes that lead the adept to their website, when scanned, for additional information, biographical and historical, plus some pictures, and additional fiction by MdA--nice feature. For those less adept, like myself, the website addy is included.</p><br /><br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1763856796" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15117875561307700137"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/22458.Machado_de_Assis' title='Machado de Assis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Machado de Assis</a> for me is one of the best Brazilian story-tellers. The novella <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13155523.The_Alienist' title='The Alienist by Machado de Assis' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Alienist</a> deals with the issue of sanity. Who is crazy and who is not? That is the central question in this story. Is Dr. Bacamarte himself sane?<br/><i>
  <blockquote>Till now, madness has been thought a small island in an ocean of sanity. I am beginning to suspect that it is not an island at all but a continent.</blockquote>
</i>Read and enjoy. Recommended.</span>
  <span id="freeText15117875561307700137" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/22458.Machado_de_Assis" title="Machado de Assis" rel="nofollow noopener">Machado de Assis</a> for me is one of the best Brazilian story-tellers. The novella <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13155523.The_Alienist" title="The Alienist by Machado de Assis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Alienist</a> deals with the issue of sanity. Who is crazy and who is not? That is the central question in this story. Is Dr. Bacamarte himself sane?<br /><i>
  <blockquote>Till now, madness has been thought a small island in an ocean of sanity. I am beginning to suspect that it is not an island at all but a continent.</blockquote>
</i>Read and enjoy. Recommended.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer820992884306763720">read twice.<br/><br/>The surface story here is great, but it's what's underneath that really speaks volumes.<br/><br/>The first few pages of this novella did not at all prepare me for what was going to come next, and continuing on, I reached the point where it felt like I was seriously entering the theater of the absurd. The story on the surface is this: one doctor in a small town in Brazil undertakes the study of mental illness, sets himself up as the expert, and proceeds to have an asylum built in the town. He an</span>
  <span id="freeText820992884306763720" style="display:none">read twice.<br /><br />The surface story here is great, but it's what's underneath that really speaks volumes.<br /><br />The first few pages of this novella did not at all prepare me for what was going to come next, and continuing on, I reached the point where it felt like I was seriously entering the theater of the absurd. The story on the surface is this: one doctor in a small town in Brazil undertakes the study of mental illness, sets himself up as the expert, and proceeds to have an asylum built in the town. He and he alone decides who is going to be committed, consequences follow. <br /><br />But in this book there's much more than merely plot to consider as is usually the case in satire; aside from the focus on mental illness and how it is determined (a commentary, I think, on the folly of relying solely on science-based reason), ideas, society and politics of the time are put squarely under the microscope here as well. I don't want to say any more than that because it certainly is a story not only worth reading, but also worth spending time pondering to pick up what's actually going on here. I loved this little book.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.readingavidly.com/2018/03/the-alienist-by-machado-de-assis.html">http://www.readingavidly.com/2018/03/...</a></span>

          </span>

        rated it

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781612191072" href="/book/show/13155523-the-alienist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4373783-dhanaraj-rajan?shelf=south-american-lit">south-american-lit</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2714113427" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4810187045088790614">Three and Half stars. Had I read it in the English translation, I believe, the rating would have gone high. I read it in the Tamil translation. It is translated by Rajagopal from its English version and is published by Sandhya Publications. The title is மனநல மருத்துவர். It is thus actually a translation of the translation.<br/><br/>I loved the story. It is about a Psychiatrist and his research on madness. How can we define madness? Usually they say that when a person loses his/her mental balance (aberrati</span>
  <span id="freeText4810187045088790614" style="display:none">Three and Half stars. Had I read it in the English translation, I believe, the rating would have gone high. I read it in the Tamil translation. It is translated by Rajagopal from its English version and is published by Sandhya Publications. The title is மனநல மருத்துவர். It is thus actually a translation of the translation.<br /><br />I loved the story. It is about a Psychiatrist and his research on madness. How can we define madness? Usually they say that when a person loses his/her mental balance (aberration from rationality), he/she is termed as mad. But the main protagonist in his research tries to expand the boundaries of this definition. How can a short tempered man be called a man with balance? How can a poet be called a balanced person? He/she certainly has illusions and speaks in no normal words. How can a person obsessed with make up be called a balanced person? Thus, the expansion of the definition of madness results in shutting almost whole of the town in the mental asylum. Later, seeing the mistake he reverses the theory. A sightly imbalanced is the new normal. So normal people are termed mad now. Certainly how can a person who forgives his/her offender be a normal person? A normal person should react violently or at least express his/her anger with swearing words. And so goes on the story.<br /><br />It is a satire on the modern day science relating to psychiatry. But to present it in an engaging story is the success of Machado de Assis.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4810187045088790614" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer569959378427610627">The Psychiatrist is a funny satire on science and its influences in society. <br/><br/>The book tells the story of obsession of a physician who desires to develop the perfect scientific method to cure mental disorders.<br/><br/>The psychiatrist opens the first asylum of city named Casa Verde (Green House) and starts to bring in citizens he judges to have a mental disorder. His theories change along the story as he studies more and more, and so changes his criteria to decide who is mentally unbalanced or not.<br/><br/>The sma</span>
  <span id="freeText569959378427610627" style="display:none">The Psychiatrist is a funny satire on science and its influences in society. <br /><br />The book tells the story of obsession of a physician who desires to develop the perfect scientific method to cure mental disorders.<br /><br />The psychiatrist opens the first asylum of city named Casa Verde (Green House) and starts to bring in citizens he judges to have a mental disorder. His theories change along the story as he studies more and more, and so changes his criteria to decide who is mentally unbalanced or not.<br /><br />The small Brazilian town collapses with fear and conspiracy as the majority of citizens start to be considered mentally ill by the scientist.<br />The novella is funny and intelligent, it is one of my favorite Machado de Assis' works.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5973086-ruby-tombstone-with-a-vengeance?shelf=treasure-hunt-scorers">treasure-hunt-scorers</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer437912683" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9141530706828942907">In the time it takes you to read a full-length review of this novella, you could have already read a substantial enough chunk of it to see for yourself how great it is. I don't want to be responsible for you losing valuable machado-reading time, so I'll just say this:<br/><br/>- It's beautifully written, nicely paced with a wry sense of wit. It didn't contain anything that made me clutch my chest in shock and my brain didn't light up with a thousand new ideas, but it is a very well crafted light farce all</span>
  <span id="freeText9141530706828942907" style="display:none">In the time it takes you to read a full-length review of this novella, you could have already read a substantial enough chunk of it to see for yourself how great it is. I don't want to be responsible for you losing valuable machado-reading time, so I'll just say this:<br /><br />- It's beautifully written, nicely paced with a wry sense of wit. It didn't contain anything that made me clutch my chest in shock and my brain didn't light up with a thousand new ideas, but it is a very well crafted light farce all the same.<br />- It's a satirical take on the mental health industry and poses the question, "Who decides who is sane and who is not?" which is guaranteed to be fertile ground.<br />- It's a very stylish edition (part of Melville House's "Art of the Novella" series) and contains a code which allows you to access a huge amount of additional content electronically, which I am still happily rummaging through.<br />-It contains little gems like this, <i>
  <b>"He possessed wisdom, patience, tolerance, truthfulness, loyalty and moral fortitude: all the qualities that go to make an utter madman."</b>
</i><br /><br />Do you really need to hear more? Go read it.<br /><br /></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3427033053" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer350271338445172309">A tiny, clever novella which is a satire of science. An alienist, or a psychologist before the title existed, wants to determine who in his small town is insane and to help those disturbed individuals by founding a home where they can live and he can treat their maladies. It was a revolutionary idea at the time and the outcome is entirely impossible to predict. <br/><br/>Satires can be biting and wicked or patently absurd and this falls squarely in the absurd camp. There is much about psychoanalysis that </span>
  <span id="freeText350271338445172309" style="display:none">A tiny, clever novella which is a satire of science. An alienist, or a psychologist before the title existed, wants to determine who in his small town is insane and to help those disturbed individuals by founding a home where they can live and he can treat their maladies. It was a revolutionary idea at the time and the outcome is entirely impossible to predict. <br /><br />Satires can be biting and wicked or patently absurd and this falls squarely in the absurd camp. There is much about psychoanalysis that is a matter of opinion. What is crazy to one person is the epitome of sanity to others and vice versa. Machado de Assis is masterful at making fun of this very idea.</span>
  <a data-text-id="350271338445172309" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1128843069" />
    <a title="Katherine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4971204-katherine"><img alt="Katherine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1501166724p2/4971204.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katherine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katherine" href="/user/show/4971204-katherine">Katherine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781612191072" href="/book/show/13155523-the-alienist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4971204-katherine?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4971204-katherine?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4971204-katherine?shelf=short-stories-novellas-and-essays">short-stories-novellas-and-essays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1128843069" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16476927624032281314">Sanity is a subject I've grown increasingly interested in as I've gotten older. I used to see it as a black and white matter. Some people are sane, others insane. Easy as that. <br/><br/>This was a cozy idea of mine as a child at least. I kept far away from the black sheep of my family - an ancient great aunt with a lifetime's wake of miserable holiday antics due to her undiagnosed narcissism - and I got used to what I perceived was textbook sanity. Although I do remember once in middle school miserably w</span>
  <span id="freeText16476927624032281314" style="display:none">Sanity is a subject I've grown increasingly interested in as I've gotten older. I used to see it as a black and white matter. Some people are sane, others insane. Easy as that. <br /><br />This was a cozy idea of mine as a child at least. I kept far away from the black sheep of my family - an ancient great aunt with a lifetime's wake of miserable holiday antics due to her undiagnosed narcissism - and I got used to what I perceived was textbook sanity. Although I do remember once in middle school miserably wishing I could be as smart as Nietzsche, as I felt too sane to ever be genius. But eventually, after those comfortable years not considering it much more, I left home. I met people comfortable with routines I could never imagine for myself, and with backgrounds equally contrasting. Not only did I question my own quirks, I feared my roots. I had grown accustomed to my Father wearing literally the same outfit every single day (à la Steve Jobs) because "it's comfortable," and to a Mother who collected rocks and scrap metals (to build a scarecrow for the backyard of course). But also around that time, humans generally became a lot more interesting to me. <br /><br />This perhaps isn't an accurate personal introduction to <i>The Alienist,</i> as it crosses into other ideas and plots - but I kept going back to this subject. Maybe I'm ready for something that addresses the topic without satire (I'm always welcome to suggestions!) for <i>The Alienist</i> is equally hilarious as it is perceptive and ahead of it's time. I'm <i>definitely</i> ready for more Machado de Assis. <br /><br /><i>Highly recommended.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="16476927624032281314" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2147895260" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2147895260" />
    <a title="Moushine Zahr" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53509133-moushine-zahr"><img alt="Moushine Zahr" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1457457507p2/53509133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2147895260?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Moushine Zahr" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Moushine Zahr" href="/user/show/53509133-moushine-zahr">Moushine Zahr</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/36197874-l-ali-niste">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2147895260" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4380851205010754776">It is the first novel I read from this author. This book was published in 1881. It reminded me of another book I read this summer titled <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23791.Ward_No__6_and_Other_Stories' title='Ward No. 6 and Other Stories by Anton Chekhov' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ward No. 6 and Other Stories</a> , which was also published in the 19th century and bears some similarities. <br/><br/>This novel is of course a psychology story without using the complex and scientific terminilogy and/or narratives. Basically, any one can read and understand this novel if you don't like psychology. It is about an emminent Doctor who creates the first place </span>
  <span id="freeText4380851205010754776" style="display:none">It is the first novel I read from this author. This book was published in 1881. It reminded me of another book I read this summer titled <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23791.Ward_No__6_and_Other_Stories" title="Ward No. 6 and Other Stories by Anton Chekhov" rel="nofollow noopener">Ward No. 6 and Other Stories</a> , which was also published in the 19th century and bears some similarities. <br /><br />This novel is of course a psychology story without using the complex and scientific terminilogy and/or narratives. Basically, any one can read and understand this novel if you don't like psychology. It is about an emminent Doctor who creates the first place to house mentally ill of a province city. Things goes well in the beginning, but easily and quickly things gets out of hand and leads to terror and rebellion within the entire society. This short novel tells the story from a human aspect about the difficulty of discerning the insane from the sane people within a society. One thing done for good or for science can lead easily to a dark side of society and evil. It is a reflection of how a democratic society can easily turn to dictatorship and how society by giving too much importance to one aspect of life such as science can lead to excess harmful to society. While the story may seem too dramatic, it is often satiric and funny in a way. <br /><br />This story was published in 1881 in Brazil makes it even more interesting to read and seems to be way ahead of its time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4380851205010754776" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1362508546" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1362508546" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19993-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485056900p2/19993.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1362508546?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/19993-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781612191072" href="/book/show/13155523-the-alienist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19993-robin?shelf=bingo">bingo</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19993-robin?shelf=novella">novella</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19993-robin?shelf=translation">translation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1362508546" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5959157773732191448">This succinct little novella was originally published in 1881 in Brazil, but feels as though it was written yesterday and only set in the past for effect. It is a satirical account of a doctor who decides to build a mental hospital so that he can collect all specimens of madness into one place, the better to study and cure them and thus "render a valuable service to humanity." As he proceeds in his work, the doctor finds himself expanding and changing his definitions of madness, to the alarm of </span>
  <span id="freeText5959157773732191448" style="display:none">This succinct little novella was originally published in 1881 in Brazil, but feels as though it was written yesterday and only set in the past for effect. It is a satirical account of a doctor who decides to build a mental hospital so that he can collect all specimens of madness into one place, the better to study and cure them and thus "render a valuable service to humanity." As he proceeds in his work, the doctor finds himself expanding and changing his definitions of madness, to the alarm of the populace and the consternation of the hapless town council.<br /><br />The scenes describing village life are full of the same lighthearted but dark humor:<blockquote>"Like the other towns, villages, and settlements in the colony at that time, Itaguai had no newspaper. It used two media for the publication of news: hand-written posters nailed to the door of the town hall and of the main church, and the noisemaker. This is how the latter medium worked: a man was hired for one or more days to go through the streets rattling a noisemaker. A crowd would gather and the man would announce whatever he had been paid to announce: a cure for malaria, a gift to the Church, some farm land for sale, and the like. He might even be engaged to read a sonnet to the people. The system continually disturbed the peace of the community, but it survived a long time because of its almost miraculous effectiveness. Incredible as it may seem, the noisemaker actually enabled merchants to sell inferior goods at superior prices and third-rate authors to pass as geniuses. Yes, indeed, not all the institutions of the old regime deserve our century's contempt."</blockquote><br /> <br />So, it was delightful, but so short that I read it in two brief sittings. I have trouble with the novella as a form and I don't quite know why. I shy away from novellas and short stories for their shortness, which puts me off. I think I want to cure myself of this and learn to appreciate shorter form factors more. Please send suggestions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5959157773732191448" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1911391895" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1911391895" />
    <a title="Jenny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21233828-jenny"><img alt="Jenny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472491337p2/21233828.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1911391895?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny" href="/user/show/21233828-jenny">Jenny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1911391895" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8422035320249486902">I read this in Portuguese, although I'm at intermediate learner level, so I probably didn't pick up on subtleties beyond the main plot. Despite this, I could still understand the dry sense of humour and 'chronicler' authorship style, which, along with the full-circle ending, made it feel like a fable. <br/><br/>It struck me as a cautionary tale ripe for today's age - an authority figure claiming that everyone else is the problem and persuading the town to pay for a large building to keep them away (naming</span>
  <span id="freeText8422035320249486902" style="display:none">I read this in Portuguese, although I'm at intermediate learner level, so I probably didn't pick up on subtleties beyond the main plot. Despite this, I could still understand the dry sense of humour and 'chronicler' authorship style, which, along with the full-circle ending, made it feel like a fable. <br /><br />It struck me as a cautionary tale ripe for today's age - an authority figure claiming that everyone else is the problem and persuading the town to pay for a large building to keep them away (naming no names).<br /><br />It's a clever idea for a story, as who would believe the protests of sanity from somebody declared insane? This creates such a feeling of injustice and powerlessness against a wilfully blind person/system.<br /><br />I enjoyed the concepts, and will read again when I've practised Portuguese some more!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8422035320249486902" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2189996709?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hope" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hope" href="/user/show/13246091-hope">Hope</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/32880532-o-alienista">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13246091-hope?shelf=light-reading">light-reading</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2189996709" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5977017106885193728">This comedy is written by one of Brazil's most famous writers. Because Portuguese is my second language, I understood about 75% of it (even with a dictionary by my side). Still, it was very funny and I imagine Brazilians would find it even funnier.<br/><br/>Simao Bacamarte is a psychiatrist who opens a clinic to study the crazy people of the city of Itaguai. As time goes on the definition of craziness is expanded to include 3/4 of the town. Mayhem ensues. Then he concludes that the real crazies are not th</span>
  <span id="freeText5977017106885193728" style="display:none">This comedy is written by one of Brazil's most famous writers. Because Portuguese is my second language, I understood about 75% of it (even with a dictionary by my side). Still, it was very funny and I imagine Brazilians would find it even funnier.<br /><br />Simao Bacamarte is a psychiatrist who opens a clinic to study the crazy people of the city of Itaguai. As time goes on the definition of craziness is expanded to include 3/4 of the town. Mayhem ensues. Then he concludes that the real crazies are not the ones who are hospitalized, but the ones who are still on the streets. He releases the lunatics and incarcerates the sane. He does his best to "desequilibrar suas faculdades" (hard to translate but literally means "to upset their reasonableness") and sends them home cured. <br /><br />This book pokes fun at societal mores, small-town governments and psychiatry, and does a splendid job of it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5977017106885193728" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2755144182" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2755144182" />
    <a title="John Pistelli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/833943-john-pistelli"><img alt="John Pistelli" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1354053455p2/833943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2755144182?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Pistelli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Pistelli" href="/user/show/833943-john-pistelli">John Pistelli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781612191072" href="/book/show/13155523-the-alienist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/833943-john-pistelli?shelf=nineteenth-century">nineteenth-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/833943-john-pistelli?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2755144182" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5050675888092072610">We could learn a lot, both about life and literature, from this 1882 novella by the Brazilian writer Joaquim Maria Machado de Assis. Machado is only now becoming prominent in the Anglophone world with Liveright's publication last year of his collected short stories (in which another translation of <em>The Alienist</em> appears; I read the 1963 version by William L. Grossmann—the first in English—as reprinted by Melville House). We are currently more aware both of his significance for Latin American lette</span>
  <span id="freeText5050675888092072610" style="display:none">We could learn a lot, both about life and literature, from this 1882 novella by the Brazilian writer Joaquim Maria Machado de Assis. Machado is only now becoming prominent in the Anglophone world with Liveright's publication last year of his collected short stories (in which another translation of <em>The Alienist</em> appears; I read the 1963 version by William L. Grossmann—the first in English—as reprinted by Melville House). We are currently more aware both of his significance for Latin American letters and of the praise he has already received from English-language authors.<br /><br />Philip Roth hailed him as a "tragic comedian," Susan Sontag commended him as "the greatest writer ever produced in Latin America," and Harold Bloom called him "the supreme black literary artist to date." If we hesitate at Sontag's import/export terminology or at Bloom's perhaps presumptuous arbitration of black aesthetics, Roth's judgment is nevertheless precise. Morten Høi Jensen, <a href="https://lareviewofbooks.org/article/mirth-and-melancholy-the-collected-stories-of-machado-de-assis/" rel="nofollow noopener">reviewing the <em>Collected Stories</em></a>, has this to say about <em>The Alienist</em>:<br /><blockquote>“The Alienist,” one of his best (and longest) stories, is a darkly comic parable of bureaucracy, madness, and power equal parts Kafka and Monty Python. I laughed out loud several times as I read it but found, upon reaching its conclusion, that what I had initially experienced as comic had become tragic. It is as if <em>Don Quixote</em> had been condensed to a 50-page novella.</blockquote><br />Though Machado is credited with bringing literary realism to Brazil, I was reminded for my part of Voltaire by the novella's slapstick briskness of narration and arch appraisal of human delusion; Jensen's evocations of Cervantes and Kafka, however, catch the pre- and post-Enlightenment undertone of despair over human incorrigibility that sounds at <em>The Alienist</em>'s close.<br /><br />At 80-some pages, <em>The Alienist</em> is a little epic of revolution and counter-revolution, a little tragedy of one man's fall from overweening ambition to final self-defeat, and a grand satire, eventually on everyone and everything. The story begins when the brilliant, European-educated physician, the eponymous psychologist Simão Barcamarte, decides, at the age of 34, to return to his provincial Brazilian home town of Itaguaí.<br /><br />There, and with the support of a local government grateful for his modernization effort, he opens the region's first asylum and, driven by pure scientific curiosity, determines to find the causes of and discover the treatments for mental illness. A rationalist, he marries a vain woman he hardly cares for because he deems her physiology (blood pressure, eyesight, etc.) promising for his progeny and claims that her charmlessness will leave him undistracted from his scientific work.<br /><br />But Barcamarte (whose name translates as "Blunderbuss") finds that more and more of his neighbors can be defined as mad; consequently, he confines more and more of them to his asylum, the Green House, so called because of its uniquely-colored windows. While Barcamarte begins with actually debilitating delusions, he comes to regard every human foible, from poetic fancy to vanity in fashion, as a symptom of mental illness and eventually has four-fifths of the population committed, including his own wife. He says:<br /><blockquote>"Till now, madness has been thought a small island in an ocean of sanity. I am beginning to suspect that is not an island at all but a continent."</blockquote><br />This incarceration of the populace provokes a revolution led by the barber Porfírio; but the new government, once installed, decides to work with Barcamarte instead of fighting him, and the previous government is restored in the end. This satire on populism, revolution, and governmental inertia suggests Machado's political cynicism: while Porfírio, pleased with his own grandiose phraseology, proposes storming the "Bastille of human reason," other fine phrases find their own constituencies, and revolution inevitably comes full circle.<br /><br />Barcamarte relents not from outside pressure but when he he begins to question his own premises: if four-fifths of people are unbalanced, then how can mental illness be considered a deviation from the norm? Perhaps the norm <em>is</em> psychological imbalance, he concludes, and only the well-adjusted and rational belong in the Green House. He puts this theory into practice, releasing his prior patients and now locking up only those who exhibit unusual sense and rectitude. When he finds that the latter are only too easy to corrupt into irrationality, he frees them too. If folly is universal, what is madness and who is the madman? The story comes to its conclusion with the elegance of a logical proof and the fatedness of a tragedy:<br /><blockquote>Simão Barcamarte...had found in himself the perfect, undeniable case of insanity. He possessed wisdom, patience, tolerance, truthfulness, loyalty, and moral fortitude—all qualities that go to make an utter madman.</blockquote><br />Why do I say we have much to learn about life and literature from this little book?<br /><br />First, life. Today we encounter an almost unprecedented faith in the medical model of the psyche. Individuals accept, and institutions increasingly not only accommodate but demand, fundamental identification based on labels devised by physicians. That these labels are themselves often tropes, names for lists of symptoms, and that their sometimes pharmacological treatments might conduce more to the profits of corporations than people—these are observations I almost hesitate to type, so concealed are they behind a moralized rhetoric of "help" and "care."<br /><br />And while I certainly don't mean to belittle the necessity of medical intervention for certain mental problems, I find <em>The Alienist</em> prophetic, especially as it was written before even the advent of psychoanalysis. Anticipating 20th-century enemies of totalizing psychology from Woolf and Nabokov to Pynchon and Foucault, Machado satirizes the arbitrariness and authoritarianism of psychological classification and queries the motives, even the sanity, of those who would presume to sit in judgment on human reason. In so doing, he speaks to the 21st century.<br /><br />But Barcamarte is not just the butt of Machado's joke—and here we find our literary lesson. In one passage, the narrator—who, Cervantes's metafictional style, claims to be summarizing a historical chronicle—contrasts Barcamarte with his assistant, the pharmacist Crispim Soares:<br /><blockquote>Crispim Soares stared at the road, between the ears of his roan. Simão Barcamarte swept the horizon with his eyes, surveyed the distant mountains, and let his horse find the way home. Perfect symbols of the common man and of the genius! One fixes his gaze upon the present with all its tears and privations; the other looks beyond to the glorious dawns of a future that he himself will shape.</blockquote><br />The humorous tone of the above notwithstanding, Barcamarte really is a genius. The proof comes when he has enough integrity to subject himself to his own theory. His true universality of perception allies him to his author, whose metafictional irony ensures that he satirizes himself and his discourse (i.e., fiction) as well as his subjects.<br /><br />Both Machado and Barcamarte are <em>disinterested</em>. This word is now lazily used as a synonym for boredom, but it properly denotes the ideal impartiality of the scientist and the artist. Like every other ideal, it is not humanly achievable, but neither is it to be abandoned. Its mark in this text is the narrator's dispassionate storytelling, a quality of withholding and understatement that allows the reader to feel the tragic finale to this ironic tale more than any authorial emoting would have.<br /><br />As Namwali Serpell notes in her polemic on <a href="https://www.nybooks.com/daily/2019/03/02/the-banality-of-empathy/" rel="nofollow noopener">"The Banality of Empathy,"</a> we may need less sentimentality in our fiction and more of the cognitive capacity to recreate viewpoints and attitudes, all the better to perceive both their potentials and their limitations. Here, too, Machado shows the way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5050675888092072610" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1028483459" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1028483459" />
    <a title="Olivia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7164733-olivia"><img alt="Olivia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1437922018p2/7164733.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1028483459?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olivia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olivia" href="/user/show/7164733-olivia">Olivia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781612191072" href="/book/show/13155523-the-alienist">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1028483459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17036879788244041105">The initial premise of The Alienist is speaking for itself: an established physician in old-time Brazil strives to contribute to the budding field of psychology, and so searches for the true definition of madness to fill his newly opened insane asylum. Building the foundations of a new science is not easy, and it takes Dr. Simão Bacamarte several more or less disastrous attempts before getting it "right". The ending is predictable, but satisfying. <br/><br/>But this is a story just as much about corruptio</span>
  <span id="freeText17036879788244041105" style="display:none">The initial premise of The Alienist is speaking for itself: an established physician in old-time Brazil strives to contribute to the budding field of psychology, and so searches for the true definition of madness to fill his newly opened insane asylum. Building the foundations of a new science is not easy, and it takes Dr. Simão Bacamarte several more or less disastrous attempts before getting it "right". The ending is predictable, but satisfying. <br /><br />But this is a story just as much about corruption as it is about clinical insanity. There is a strong underlying theme of political satire going on here, pointing to corruption as something inevitable, as not only a possible side effect to power, but as a direct consequence of it. de Assis himself was very skeptical of the politics of his time, and in The Alienist, he shows a general mistrust in political change. One coup d'etat is followed by another, but the politics are all the same. The ever-changing political climate seems to shift only superficially. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[When one dictator/protector replaces another, he even uses the a copy of the latters proclamation to the people. Has any actual change taken place? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Here, human nature dooms every imbalance of power to turn abusive (which is interesting, seeing as de Assis was a staunch supporter of monarchism).<br /><br />Fortunately for The Alienist, and unfortunately for us, the satire still works. It remains as relevant as ever, feeling almost like a parable, forever adapting to modern times. The way the book is written does in fact resemble an old folk tale, a bit polished up but with that same dramatic quality of hearsay given by the many inclusions of sentences such as "according to the chroniclers..." and "we need not specifically note...". It lends a refreshing simplicity to a story that could easily have turned convoluted. The humour is absolutely wonderful. It is dry and scornful and takes no prisoners. Nobody is spared the chilly glare of de Assis, but ultimately, it makes the characters feel more human. The author isn't pointing fingers at a series of grotesque individuals, but at the imperfections that we all possess. Everybody is abnormal, and nobody is. <br /><br />Even more refreshing, The Alienist isn't afraid to stray a bit from Bacamarte, our main character. Instead of following his every step and breathing down his neck, we get to see the influence that he has over other people's circumstances, and the impact he makes on the town of Itaguai. And de Assis makes the whole town shine. Every single character feels so alive. They all exist to contribute to the story and the feel of the book, instead of just serving as a background to Bacamarte. I actually felt like this was the most accomplished part of the entire piece. With just a few sentences, de Assis manages to take what could have ended up as two-dimensional figures and gives them depth, gifting them with unique personalities, their own thoughts and their own intentions. This way of highlighting a handful of people instead of only one or two fits oh so well with the format the author is writing in. Speaking of the format, it is the perfect length. No space is wasted, nothing feels left out. The Alienist makes me want to hunt down the rest of de Assis' books and see if writing this good is a habit of his. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17036879788244041105" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2033214953" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2033214953" />
    <a title="Jeff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23512183-jeff"><img alt="Jeff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378221106p2/23512183.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2033214953?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff" href="/user/show/23512183-jeff">Jeff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781612191072" href="/book/show/13155523-the-alienist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23512183-jeff?shelf=fiction-literary">fiction-literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23512183-jeff?shelf=books-in-translation">books-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23512183-jeff?shelf=indie-press">indie-press</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2033214953" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10118510857847567869">"He had found in himself the perfect, undeniable case of insanity. He possessed wisdom, patience, tolerance, truthfulness, loyalty, and moral fortitude—all the qualities that go to make an utter madman."<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1498659424i/23149004.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>There's an episode of Cheers where affable town drunk, Norm, gets a promotion at his job. (It's one of those rare Cheers story lines that provides any indication of life outside the bar.) Because of his kind nature, Norm is tasked with being the corporate "hatchet man," meaning it's up to him to </span>
  <span id="freeText10118510857847567869" style="display:none">"He had found in himself the perfect, undeniable case of insanity. He possessed wisdom, patience, tolerance, truthfulness, loyalty, and moral fortitude—all the qualities that go to make an utter madman."<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1498659424i/23149004.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>There's an episode of Cheers where affable town drunk, Norm, gets a promotion at his job. (It's one of those rare Cheers story lines that provides any indication of life outside the bar.) Because of his kind nature, Norm is tasked with being the corporate "hatchet man," meaning it's up to him to call other people up and let them know they've been fired in a way that softens the blow. It's such difficult emotional labor that he cries every time he tells somebody the news, turning the tables and resulting in them comforting him. But eventually he gets used to it, the tears no longer come when doing the axing, and Norm realizes that he's becoming a harder, meaner person because of this role. He tries calling the executives to quit, but by now everyone at the company knows that a call from Norm means one is about to be fired so no one will take his call. He can't get his boss or any of the higher ups on the phone because they are all afraid of him.<br><br>I don't remember how the Cheers episode ends, but the point is I thought of it after reading the lovely and hysterical book, The Alienist. It's a comic novella, for sure, but it's also a parable about mass panic, social influence, and the way fear can lead to a quick aggregation of power. It's also a clever philosophical musing on the nature of sanity and whether the concepts of "normal" and "abnormal" have any meaning absent an enforced cultural context.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10118510857847567869" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5580348506078577563" style="display:none">Very interesting. Much easier to read than his novels I started on, I think mostly because of the reduced scope and slightly faster plot, rather than the language style. (On the other hand, the vocabulary still tripped me up on nearly every page.)<br /> Of course I had to read this book since I'm a psychiatrist who's lived in Brazil. It would be better as a very short short story, but it was still thought-provoking despite the absurd exaggeration. I have written elsewhere about the validity and utility of (modern) psychiatric diagnosis, and that information together with the absurdity combined so none of that stuff really annoyed me. But the protagonist who let his medical-scientific career blind him to the important things in life, and to his own flaws--that gave me pause. <br /> Recommended for period literature more than for its ideas, at least until after you've read the "elsewhere" mentioned above. :)<br /> Black KJ: Psychiatry and the medical model. Pp. 3-14 (ch. 1) in Rubin EH, Zorumski CF: Adult Psychiatry. (Blackwell’s Neurology and Psychiatry Access series [no volume number], series editor David RB.) Bristol, U.K.: Blackwell, 2005.<br /> Finished date approximate.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6815673037770488196">Four and a half stars. There are a couple of minor, enjoyable, twists (or, turns, I suppose) in the plot, but the end of this novella is pretty predictable.   <span id="freeText6815673037770488196" style="display:none">Four and a half stars. There are a couple of minor, enjoyable, twists (or, turns, I suppose) in the plot, but the end of this novella is pretty predictable. Luckily, that doesn't really matter because this is a satire, and it's written at just the right level for me--I think I got most of the humour, but it wasn't so obvious that I couldn't feel smug about it. It almost twinkled, I thought.<br /><br />Apparently this de Assis guy is one of the greatest Brazilian writers ever, and I'm definitely interested in reading more of his stuff. Additionally, I read this novella as part of the Melville House subscription series to their Art of the Novella, and it was delivered along with Tolstoy's "The Devil". I'm not sure how Melville house arranges their selections, but the last line of "The Devil" was an appropriate complement to "The Alienist": "The most mentally deranged people are certainly those who see in others indications of insanity they do not notice in themselves."</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13275890091146902200">Amusing tale from one of Brazil's classically revered C19th authors. A brilliant and meticulously scientific Brazilian scientist returns from great success and fame in Europe to set up shop as an one of those new alienists in his small home town. However, he has to decide where the line between sanity and insanity falls as he fills his new clinic with patients. Then the dwindling numbers free citizens in the township revolt... <br/>To a modern reader, a predictable enough ending, but with lots of nic</span>
  <span id="freeText13275890091146902200" style="display:none">Amusing tale from one of Brazil's classically revered C19th authors. A brilliant and meticulously scientific Brazilian scientist returns from great success and fame in Europe to set up shop as an one of those new alienists in his small home town. However, he has to decide where the line between sanity and insanity falls as he fills his new clinic with patients. Then the dwindling numbers free citizens in the township revolt... <br />To a modern reader, a predictable enough ending, but with lots of nice lines and jokes to boost it to four points. <br />Example, on the Dr's choice of wife: "If, in addition to her physiological accomplishments [her urinalysis was negative, for instance], Dona Evarista possessed a face composed of features neither individually pretty nor mutually compatible, he thanked God for it, for he would not be tempted to sacrifice scientific pursuits to the contemplation of his wife's attractions." </span>
